        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366"><meta property="og:title" content="Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/acs.nanolett.7b01366.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium-ion battery technologies are known to suffer from kinetic problems associated with the solid-state diffusion of Na+ in intercalation electrodes, which results in suppressed specific capacity and degraded rate performance. Here, a controllable selective etching approach is developed for the synthesis of Prussian blue analogue (PBA) with enhanced sodium storage activity. On the basis of time-dependent experiments, a defect-induced morphological evolution mechanism from nanocube to nanoflower structure is proposed. Through in situ X-ray diffraction measurement and computational analysis, this unique structure is revealed to provide higher Na+ diffusion dynamics and negligible volume change during the sodiation/desodiation processes. As a sodium ion battery cathode, the PBA exhibits a discharge capacity of 90 mA h g–1, which is in good agreement with the complete low spin FeLS(C) redox reaction. It also demonstrates an outstanding rate capability of 71.0 mA h g–1 at 44.4 C, as well as an unprecedented cycling reversibility over 5000 times."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/acs.nanolett.7b01366.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187fb7be29075b-MAN"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187fb7be29075b-MAN">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'ca4fcff2-5624-40fd-a36c-92032ddfe8b8'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187fb7be29075b-MAN">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'nano_letters';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/nano_letters/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/nano_letters/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-getftr-references-enabled="data-getFTR-references-enabled" data-getftr-reference-url="/action/CitationEntitlement" data-getftr-citedby-enabled="data-getFTR-citedBy-enabled" data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite This:</strong></span></a><span class="article_header-cite-this figure-viewer-cite-this"><span class="cit-fg-title"><i>Nano Lett.</i></span><span <script nonce="93187fb7be29075b-MAN">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "nalefd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Activation of Sodium Storage Sites in Prussian Blue Analogues via Surface Etching", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">262</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="f_tgr1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01366b_0004.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;id=f_tgr1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium-ion battery technologies are known to suffer from kinetic problems associated with the solid-state diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> in intercalation electrodes, which results in suppressed specific capacity and degraded rate performance. Here, a controllable selective etching approach is developed for the synthesis of Prussian blue analogue (PBA) with enhanced sodium storage activity. On the basis of time-dependent experiments, a defect-induced morphological evolution mechanism from nanocube to nanoflower structure is proposed. Through in situ X-ray diffraction measurement and computational analysis, this unique structure is revealed to provide higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics and negligible volume change during the sodiation/desodiation processes. As a sodium ion battery cathode, the PBA exhibits a discharge capacity of 90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, which is in good agreement with the complete low spin Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox reaction. It also demonstrates an outstanding rate capability of 71.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 44.4 C, as well as an unprecedented cycling reversibility over 5000 times.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D4713%26pageCount%3D6%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DWenhao%2BRen%252C%2BMingsheng%2BQin%252C%2BZixuan%2BZhu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D17%26issueNum%3D8%26contentID%3Dacs.nanolett.7b01366%26title%3DActivation%2Bof%2BSodium%2BStorage%2BSites%2Bin%2BPrussian%2BBlue%2BAnalogues%2Bvia%2BSurface%2BEtching%26numPages%3D6%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1530-6984%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Dnalefd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D4718%26publicationDate%3DAugust%2B2017" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2017 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure"> Chemical structure </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290830&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Diffusion"> Diffusion </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291719&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Redox reactions"> Redox reactions </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Activation&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Activation">Activation</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Prussian+Blue+Analogues&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_Prussian%20blue%20analogues">Prussian blue analogues</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Etching&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_etching">etching</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium+Ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium%20ion%20batteries">sodium ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Solid-state+Diffusion&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_solid-state%20diffusion">solid-state diffusion</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">To meet the substantially increased energy consumptions, low-cost and long lifespan battery techniques are urgently needed for the electrical grid. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1-4)</a> Considering the elemental properties as well as abundance, the most promising substitute to the lithium ion battery is the sodium ion battery (SIB). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 5 to 8">(5-8)</a> However, SIBs suffer from sluggish dynamics linked to the large ionic radius of Na<sup>+</sup>, leading to the suppressed capacity and limited rate performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9, 10)</a> For this reason, Prussian blue analogues (PBAs, A<sub><i>x</i></sub>M<sub>1</sub>[M<sub>2</sub>(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub>·<i>n</i>H<sub>2</sub>O: A: alkaline metal; M: transition metal; 0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 2; <i>y</i> ≤ 1) have attracted growing attentions owing to their intrinsic open framework structure, high theoretical capacity, and large interstitial sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11-13)</a> The room-temperature synthesis procedure and low cost of PBAs are also extremely beneficial toward large-scale applications. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14, 15)</a> However, PBAs suffer from insufficiently utilized low spin (LS) Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox couples associated with suppressed Na<sup>+</sup> mobility in bulk structure, resulting in degraded discharge capacity and limited rate capability. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a></p><p>Many efforts have been applied to improve the activity of Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) sodium storage sites. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 17 to 19">(17-19)</a> The Goodenough group <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> reported a novel rhombohedral Prussian white (Na<sub>1.92</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]) with enhanced electrochemical performance as the SIB cathode, but a low capacity contribution of Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) reaction was still observed especially under high rate. Jiang et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> created a facile approach to synthesize the ketjen black decorated PB composite, which exhibited enhanced Na<sup>+</sup> storage properties for Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) sites. Unfortunately, most of the previous efforts failed to take full advantage of the Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox couple (theoretical capacity: ∼90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>). New approaches to activate the sodium storage sites associated with Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) reaction are urgently needed. To make full use of redox sites, one of the optimal approaches is surface activation since most of the intercalation processes follow the outside-in route. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 21 and 22">(21, 22)</a> Different from carbon decoration or polymer coating, the surface activation is a process of recrystallization, which not only increases the number of active sites on the surface, but also promotes the activity of reaction site in the central area. Nevertheless, bulk synthesis of PBAs has long been performed via a coprecipitation process, and few researches have led to the surface activation. Therefore, efficient methods for the fabrication of surface activated PBAs are of great significance.</p><p>Herein, we report a new surface etching approach to activate the sodium storage sites in the nickel hexacyanoferrate (NiHCF) cathode, which displays a unique nanoflower structure. The formation mechanism of this structure is identified as a defect-induced heterogeneous reaction process. Electrochemical results clearly reveal that the surface activated NiHCF exhibits the complete reaction of Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) sites, superior rate capability, and unprecedented cycling stability. The structure–property relationships of NiHCF are well-understood by the outside-in diffusion diagrams combined with the computational analysis, which suggests that the nanoflower structure provides a faster Na<sup>+</sup> transport kinetics compared with the nanocube among the whole sodiation/desodiation processes.</p><p>The etched NiHCF (NiHCF-etch) was prepared by two steps, from the coprecipitation synthesis of NiHCF-cube (NiHCF-cube) to the following alkaline corrosion process. Schematic illustrations accompanied by time-dependent experiments were carried out to clarify the morphological evolution of NiHCF during the etching process (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The pristine NiHCF-cubes are highly uniform with an average size of 300 nm and have sharp edges and corners (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a-1). After incubating in a sodium hydroxide solution for 0.5 h, the edges of NiHCF-cubes were initially dissolved due to the disassembly of Fe–C≡N–Ni bond, whereas the plane surfaces remain unchanged (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a-2). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> It is known that the etching process of PBAs follows the defect concentration induced inhomogeneous reaction, and these defects can be mainly ascribed to the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancies. The edges or corners of PBAs are less stable compared with the flat planes, which is consistent with previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Then, the etched area extended preferably along each face, and the nanosheet structure emerged on the surface of cubes, which may be attributed to the dissolution–recrystallization processes. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref25" aria-label="reference 24 and 25">(24, 25)</a> Finally, more nanosheets were generated, and the nanoflower structure was formed when the reaction time reached 6 h (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>a-3). Meanwhile, the surface of NiHCF-etch was entirely reassembled, and the size of internal cubes was 100–200 nm. When the etching time was prolonged to 10 h, the inner nanocubes were almost etched away, and the cumulated nanosheet structure was obtained (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>).</p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01366b_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagrams accompanied by field-emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) images of the NiHCF products after the etching time for (a-1) 0 h, (a-2) 0.5 h, (a-3) 6 h. (b) X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns, (c) Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) surface area and (d) X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>From XRD measurement and the inductively coupled plasma (ICP) analysis, the as-synthesized NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch are taken to be rhombohedral Na<sub>0.96</sub>NiHCF·1.02H<sub>2</sub>O and Na<sub>1.11</sub>NiHCF·0.71H<sub>2</sub>O (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>). The increase of Na contents in NiHCF-etch is attributed to the concentration diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> from sodium citrate/sodium hydroxide solution into the structure accompanied by the etching process. The atomic ratio of Fe/Ni in NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch are 0.92 and 0.80, respectively, indicating the decomposition of Fe–C≡N–Ni bond and loss of Fe<sup>2+</sup> during etching, which is consistent with previous report. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Besides, the refined XRD results by Rietveld method (Topas software) indicate that the lattice parameter α increased from 6.338 Å for NiHCF-etch to 6.344 Å for the NiHCF-cube sample. The crystal structure of NiHCF is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, where high-spin (HS) Ni ions are coordinated to nitrogen atoms and low-spin (LS) Fe ions are coordinated to carbon atoms, forming a three-dimensional network with Ni<sup>II</sup>–N≡C–Fe<sup>II</sup>chains. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 26 and 27">(26, 27)</a> The BET surface area also increased from 12.7 to 38.0 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> after the formation of nanoflower structure (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>c). A small content of water is assessed for NiHCF-etch (0.71 H<sub>2</sub>O/f.u.) compared with NiHCF-cube (1.02 H<sub>2</sub>O/f.u.) based on the weight loss in the TGA curve (50 &lt; <i>T</i> &lt; 200 °C, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Then, the XPS, Raman, and Fourier transform infrared spectra (FTIR) were carried out to identify the valence state and chemical bonding interaction of NiHCF (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig1" aria-label="Figure 1">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>d, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S5 and S6</a>) before and after etching. All of the results show that the valence state of Fe and Ni are +2, and no bonding variation except the slight hydroxylation of the surface is observed. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref29 ref30" aria-label="reference 28 to 30">(28-30)</a></p><p>To investigate the electrochemical performance of electrodes, sodium half cells were fabricated. The redox properties of the two samples were explored by cyclic voltammetry (CV) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. A pair of well-defined redox peaks at ∼3.4 V appear in NiHCF-etch, corresponding to the equilibrium voltage of Fe<sup>II</sup>/Fe<sup>III</sup>. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> Moreover, NiHCF-etch has a larger curve area and higher redox peak current compared with NiHCF-cube, indicating that the NiHCF-etch possesses higher specific capacity as well as faster kinetics for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The initial galvanostatic charge–discharge curves of the samples at 1.1 C (1 C = 90 mA g<sup>–1</sup>) are presented (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). It is known that the Coulombic efficiencies of PBAs at the first cycle are generally below the 100%, which is attributed to the interstitial water. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref33" aria-label="reference 33">(33)</a> Therefore, the enhanced Coulombic efficiency of NiHCF-etch compared with NiHCF-cube may be assigned to the smaller content of interstitial water as confirmed by TGA (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>). Notably, the specific capacity of NiHCF-etch (∼90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>) at the second cycle (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b) can be well-identified as the complete reaction of Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox couples, which corresponds to the insertion/extraction of almost 1.0 Na/f.u., considerably exceeding the previous results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). By contrast, the specific capacity of the NiHCF-cube is only 66 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> (0.73 Na/f.u.) with a higher overpotential. In addition, the rate capabilities of NiHCF-etch and NiHCF-cube were also evaluated ranging from 1.1 C to an ultrahigh rate of 44.4 C (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). The NiHCF-etch exhibits a substantial capacity of 70.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> even at the ultrahigh rate of 44.4 C (60 s for a charge or a discharge process), corresponding to 78.8% retention of the capacity at 1.1 C, whereas the discharge capacities of NiHCF-cube are only 66.8 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1.1 C and 5.9 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 44.4 C. The long-term structural durability of the electrodes at 5.5 C was also investigated (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig2" aria-label="Figure 2">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). Notably, the capacity retentions of NiHCF-etch and NiHCF-cube reach up to 83.2% and 76.9% after 5000 cycles, and the Coulombic efficiency remains ∼100% in the overall battery operation, suggesting excellent reversibility. This is the longest cycle life of PBAs in SIBs ever reported, and such enhanced performance demonstrates the success of surface activation strategy.</p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01366b_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) CV profiles and (b) charge–discharge curves of NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch at the second cycle. (c) Rate performance and (d) cycling stability of NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The solid-state diffusion process of Na<sup>+</sup> in electrodes follows the outside-in route (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a), and their mobility becomes the limiting factor for an electrode reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> To utilize all of the reaction sites in the NiHCF-cube (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-1), Na<sup>+</sup> ions are required to pass through a long distance from surface into center, leading to the suppressed reactivity in the central part. Meanwhile, for the NiHCF-etch, Na<sup>+</sup> ions merely need to diffuse through a much shorter distance so as to occupy all of the insertion sites (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a-2), and a large proportion of the reaction sites in central area may be accessed. Furthermore, a galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was employed to analyze the solid-state diffusion kinetics of Na<sup>+</sup> (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b). The electrodes were first cycled at 1.1 C for three times to reach the normal operation state prior to GITT test. The chemical diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>) as a function of stoichiometry (<i>x</i>) after normalization were computationally analyzed according to the GITT (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S8</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The calculated <i>D</i><sup>GITT</sup> for the NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch is between 10<sup>–10</sup>–10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and 10<sup>–9</sup>–10<sup>–10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. A similar trend is observed for both electrodes with a minimum <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub> at <i>x</i> ≈ 1 (sodiated state), at which the <i>D</i><sup>GITT</sup> of the NiHCF-cube drops rapidly to about 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, indicating a sluggish electrochemical kinetics along with insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. By contrast, the <i>D</i><sup>GITT</sup> of the NiHCF-etch does not decline significantly after the sodiation process, which is in good agreement with the outside-in diffusion mechanism presented in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a. CV <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36 ref37" aria-label="reference 36 and 37">(36, 37)</a> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> (EIS, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) tests were also conducted to calculate <i>D</i><sub>Na</sub>, and the results are consistent with GITT analysis. To further verify the above calculations, the GITT and EIS analyses based on lithium batteries were also carried out (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>). Similar to the sodiated/desodiated processes, the diffusivity of NiHCF-etch versus Li<sup>+</sup>/Li is also higher than NiHCF-cube at each state (0 ≤ <i>x</i> ≤ 1), indicating the faster soild-state diffusion of alkali cations in the NiHCF-etch.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/medium/nl-2017-01366b_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Outside-in diffusion route of Na<sup>+</sup> in NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch. (b) The GITT curves of NiHCF-cube and NiHCF-etch after normalization. (c) The calculated diffusion coefficients versus state of discharge. (d) In situ XRD pattern of NiHCF-etch.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/asset/images/large/nl-2017-01366b_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>The electrochemical reaction mechanism of NiHCF-etch was investigated through in situ XRD tests with Co Kα X-ray source (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#fig3" aria-label="Figure 3">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d). The employ of Co Kα X-ray source can effectively avoid the signal interference by Fe or Ni. Remarkably, the peak shift in XRD patterns is negligible during the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction process, which means the lattice parameters of the NiHCF-etch remain almost unchanged. This redox reaction is advantageous because the crystal structure of NiHCF-etch is avoided from large volume variation and stress–strain during the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction, and thus prevents the collapse of the crystal framework. With the decrease of Na contents, the crystal structure of the NiHCF-etch gradually evolves from rhombohedral to cubic, demonstrating that the low Na concentration improves the crystal symmetry. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Conversely, the structure change is highly reversible, and peaks return to the original positions after the complete insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. The ex situ XRD analysis was also performed on NiHCF-cube, and all of the peaks exhibit the same trend with the in situ XRD results (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a>). The overall electrochemical reaction is based on LS-Fe<sup>2+</sup> (<i>t</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>6</sup><i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>) ∼ LS-Fe<sup>3+</sup> (<i>t</i><sub>2g</sub><sup>5</sup><i>e</i><sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>) and can be described by following expression: rhombohedral Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Ni<sup>II</sup>Fe<sup>II</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> ↔ cubic Na<sub><i>x</i>–1</sub>Ni<sup>II</sup>Fe<sup>III</sup>(CN)<sub>6</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S13</a>).</p><p class="last">In summary, a surface-activated NiHCF-etch cathode with fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics is obtained through a preferential etching strategy. Based on time-dependent experiments, a defect-induced morphological evolution mechanism from nanocube to nanoflower is proposed. Compared with previous reports, the NiHCF-etch overcomes the problem of underutilized Fe<sup>LS</sup>(C) redox sites. It exhibits a specific capacity of 90 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, an extremely high rate capability of 71.0 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 44.4 C, and the unprecedented cycle life over 5000 times, enabling the NiHCF-etch a promising cathode for a long lifespan and high-power SIBs. Besides, the structure–property relationships of the NiHCF-etch have been well-understood via the diffusion diagrams combined with the computational analysis, which indicates that the surface activation is able to greatly improve the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion dynamics. It is expected that this new surface activation strategy can also be applied to construct other materials with a faster ionic mobility.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i1">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366" class="ext-link">10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Experimental section and additional figures (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366/suppl_file/nl7b01366_si_001.pdf">nl7b01366_si_001.pdf (1.79 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acs.nanolett.7b01366" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards greener and more sustainable batteries for electrical energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Larcher, D.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Chemistry</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Ever-growing energy needs and depleting fossil-fuel resources demand the pursuit of sustainable energy alternatives, including both renewable energy sources and sustainable storage technologies.  It is therefore essential to incorporate material abundance, eco-efficient synthetic processes and life-cycle anal. into the design of new electrochem. storage systems.  At present, a few existing technologies address these issues, but in each case, fundamental and technol. hurdles remain to be overcome.  Here the authors provide an overview of the current state of energy storage from a sustainability perspective.  The authors introduce the notion of sustainability through discussion of the energy and environmental costs of state-of-the-art Li-ion batteries, considering elemental abundance, toxicity, synthetic methods and scalability.  With the same themes in mind, the authors also highlight current and future electrochem. storage systems beyond Li-ion batteries.  The complexity and importance of recycling battery materials is also discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nl403669a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, R.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The reversible insertion of monovalent ions such as lithium into electrode materials has enabled the development of rechargeable batteries with high energy d.  Reversible insertion of divalent ions such as magnesium would allow the creation of new battery chemistries that are potentially safer and cheaper than lithium-based batteries.  Here we report that nanomaterials in the Prussian Blue family of open framework materials, such as nickel hexacyanoferrate, allow for the reversible insertion of aq. alk. earth divalent ions, including Mg2+, Ca2+, Sr2+, and Ba2+.  We show unprecedented long cycle life and high rate performance for divalent ion insertion.  Our results represent a step forward and pave the way for future development in divalent batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/smll.201604181"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ren, W.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhu, Z.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">An, Q.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Mai, L.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Small</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">13</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1604181</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1604193</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Flexible transparent molybdenum trioxide nanopaper for energy storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yao, Bin; Huang, Liang; Zhang, Jing; Gao, Xiang; Wu, Jiabin; Cheng, Yongliang; Xiao, Xu; Wang, Bo; Li, Yat; Zhou, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">28</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">30</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">6353-6358</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ADVMEW</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0935-9648</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The authors successfully synthesized well-dispersed, ultralong molybdenum trioxide nanobelts with an av. length of 200 μm via a hydrothermal method.  A flexible transparent nanopaper via the assembly of theses ultralong nanobelts displays an excellent av. transmittance of 90% in the visible region.  The free standing nanopaper electrode delivers an outstanding specific capacitance of 1198 F g-1 at 2 mV s-1, which is just slightly below the theor. value of molybdenum trioxide.  The excellent specific capacitance can be ascribed to the efficient ion diffusion in the 3-dimensional structure and the very thin nanobelts, as well as the reduced distance of electron transport from the transparent nanopaper electrode to current collector.  The nanopaper-based supercapacitor device also shows an excellent long term stability performance over 20000 cycles with a retention rate of 96.5%.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1007/s12274-016-1300-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Wang, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Lin, D.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhao, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Liu, C.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xie, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cui, Y.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Res.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">10</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1213</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1222</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Prussian blue route to nitrogen-doped graphene aerogels as efficient electrocatalysts for oxygen reduction with enhanced active site accessibility</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yayuan; Wang, Haotian; Lin, Dingchang; Zhao, Jie; Liu, Chong; Xie, Jin; Cui, Yi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Research</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1213-1222</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NRAEB5</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1998-0000</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer GmbH</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Developing high-performance nonprecious-metal electrocatalysts for the oxygen redn. reaction (ORR) is crucial for a variety of renewable energy conversion and storage systems.  Toward that end, rational catalyst design principles that lead to highly active catalytic centers and enhanced active site accessibility are undoubtedly of paramount importance.  Here, we used Prussian blue nanoparticles to anchor Fe/Fe3C species to nitrogen-doped reduced graphene oxide aerogels as ORR catalysts.  The strong interaction between nanosized Fe3C and the graphitic carbon shell led to synergistic effects in the ORR, and the protection of the carbon shell guaranteed stability of the catalyst.  As a result, the aerogel electrocatalyst displayed outstanding activity in the ORR on par with the state-of-the-art Pt/C catalyst at the same mass loading in alk. media, good performance in acidic media, and excellent stability and crossover tolerance that rivaled that of the best nonprecious-metal ORR electrocatalysts reported to date.[Figure not available: see fulltextc.].</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yabuuchi, N.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kubota, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Dahbi, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Komaba, S.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Chem. Soc.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">135</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">1167</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">1176</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1021/ja3091438</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar%3Fhl%3Den%26q%3DGoodenough%252C%2BJ.%2BB.%253B%2BPark%252C%2BK.%2BS.J.%2BAm.%2BChem.%2BSoc.%2B2013%252C%2B135%252C%2B1167%25E2%2580%25931176&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facs.nanolett.7b01366&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fja3091438&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">The Li-Ion Rechargeable Battery: A Perspective</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.; Park, Kyu-Sung</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the American Chemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">135</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1167-1176</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JACSAT</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0002-7863</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Each cell of a battery stores elec. energy as chem. energy in two electrodes, a reductant (anode) and an oxidant (cathode), sepd. by an electrolyte that transfers the ionic component of the chem. reaction inside the cell and forces the electronic component outside the battery.  The output on discharge is an external electronic current I at a voltage V for a time Δt.  The chem. reaction of a rechargeable battery must be reversible on the application of a charging I and V.  Crit. parameters of a rechargeable battery are safety, d. of energy that can be stored at a specific power input and retrieved at a specific power output, cycle and shelf life, storage efficiency, and cost of fabrication.  Conventional ambient-temp. rechargeable batteries have solid electrodes and a liq. electrolyte.  The pos. electrode (cathode) consists of a host framework into which the mobile (working) cation is inserted reversibly over a finite solid-soln. range.  The solid-soln. range, which is reduced at higher current by the rate of transfer of the working ion across electrode/electrolyte interfaces and within a host, limits the amt. of charge per electrode formula unit that can be transferred over the time Δt = Δt(I).  Moreover, the difference between energies of the LUMO and the HOMO of the electrolyte, i.e., electrolyte window, dets. the max. voltage for a long shelf and cycle life.  The max. stable voltage with an aq. electrolyte is 1.5 V; the Li-ion rechargeable battery uses an org. electrolyte with a larger window, which increase the d. of stored energy for a given Δt.  Anode or cathode electrochem. potentials outside the electrolyte window can increase V, but they require formation of a passivating surface layer that must be permeable to Li+ and capable of adapting rapidly to the changing electrode surface area as the electrode changes vol. during cycling.  A passivating surface layer adds to the impedance of the Li+ transfer across the electrode/electrolyte interface and lowers the cycle life of a battery cell.  Moreover, formation of a passivation layer on the anode robs Li from the cathode irreversibly on an initial charge, further lowering the reversible Δt.  These problems plus the cost of quality control of manufg. plague development of Li-ion rechargeable batteries that can compete with the internal combustion engine for powering elec. cars and that can provide the needed low-cost storage of elec. energy generated by renewable wind and/or solar energy.  Chemists are contributing to incremental improvements of the conventional strategy by investigating and controlling electrode passivation layers, improving the rate of Li+ transfer across electrode/electrolyte interfaces, identifying electrolytes with larger windows while retaining a Li+ cond. σLi &gt; 10-3 S cm-1, synthesizing electrode morphologies that reduce the size of the active particles while pinning them on current collectors of large surface area accessible by the electrolyte, lowering the cost of cell fabrication, designing displacement-reaction anodes of higher capacity that allow a safe, fast charge, and designing alternative cathode hosts.  However, new strategies are needed for batteries that go beyond powering hand-held devices, such as using electrode hosts with two-electron redox centers; replacing the cathode hosts by materials that undergo displacement reactions (e.g. sulfur) by liq. cathodes that may contain flow-through redox mols., or by catalysts for air cathodes; and developing a Li+ solid electrolyte separator membrane that allows an org. and aq. liq. electrolyte on the anode and cathode sides, resp.  Opportunities exist for the chemist to bring together oxide and polymer or graphene chem. in imaginative morphologies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/nl302618s"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xu, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cao, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Jeong, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Cho, J.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Nano Lett.</span><span class="NLM_x"> </span><span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2012</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_volume">12</span><span class="NLM_x">, </span> <span class="NLM_fpage">4988</span><span class="NLM_x">–</span> <span class="NLM_lpage">4991</span><span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Spindle-like Mesoporous α-Fe2O3 Anode Material Prepared from MOF Template for High-Rate Lithium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xiaodong; Cao, Ruiguo; Jeong, Sookyung; Cho, Jaephil</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

When tested as anode material for lithium batteries (LBs), this spindle-like porous α-Fe2O3 shows greatly enhanced performance of Li storage. Even when cycled at 10 C, comparable capacity of 424 mAh g-1 could be achieved.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201501527"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Xiang, X.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Zhang, K.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Chen, J.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Adv.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Crystallographic-plane tuned Prussian-blue wrapped with RGO: a high-capacity, long-life cathode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Hui; Wang, Li; Chen, Shuangming; Li, Guopeng; Quan, Junjie; Xu, Enze; Song, Li; Jiang, Yang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3569-3577</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Designing Prussian blue with optimally exposed crystal planes and confining it in a conductive matrix are crit. issues for improving its sodium storage performance, and will result in much improved sodium ion adsorption and diffusion, together with improved electron mobility.  Here, we firstly illustrate through DFT simulations that the {100} lattice planes and [100] direction of the KxFeFe(CN)6 crystal are the preferred occupation sites and diffusion route for sodium ions.  In addn., through coupling with RGO, KxFeFe(CN)6 electrodes exhibit better electronic cond.  Accordingly, {100} plane-capped cubic K0.33FeFe(CN)6 wrapped in RGO was fabricated using a facile CTAB-assisted method.  Due to the highly robust framework, higher sp. surface area, greatly reduced no. of lattice water defects and conductive RGO coating, K0.33FeFe(CN)6/RGO exhibits superior electrochem. performance in sodium-ion batteries.  As a cathode, the RGO-coated K0.33FeFe(CN)6 yields an initial discharge-charge capacity of 160 mA h g-1 at a rate of 0.5C, and an excellent capacity retention of 92.2% at 0.5C and 90.1% at 10C after 1000 and 500 cycles.  Furthermore, x-ray diffraction, DFT simulation, XANES and EXAFS verified that the structural changes during the Na-ion insertion-extn. processes are highly reversible.  All these results suggest that {100} plane-capped K0.33FeFe(CN)6/RGO has excellent potential as a cathode for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref14"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref14" class="refNumLink">14</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit14" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201105190"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Hu, M.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Furukawa, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Ohtani, R.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Sukegawa, H.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Nemoto, Y.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Reboul, J.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Kitagawa, S.</span><span class="NLM_x">; </span><span class="NLM_contrib-group">Yamauchi, Y.</span> <span class="citation_source-journal">Angew.
